				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>

							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Difference in pitch between two sounds</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For albums or bands named Intervals, see <a href="/wiki/Interval_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Interval (disambiguation)">Interval (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:-moz-fit-content; width:fit-content;"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:auto;">
<div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Melodic_and_harmonic_intervals.mid"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/k/3/k3juf9xtecizrmy0vex01b7193ug9m8/k3juf9xt.png" width="246" height="136" alt="&#10;\layout {&#10;line-width = 60\mm&#10;indent = 0\mm&#10;}&#10;\relative c&#39;&#39;{&#10;\clef treble \time 3/1 \hide Staff.TimeSignature&#10;d,1 g f \bar &quot;||&quot; \break&#10;\time 1/1 &lt;d f&gt; \bar &quot;||&quot; &lt;d g&gt; \bar &quot;||&quot; &lt;f g&gt; \bar &quot;||&quot;&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/override-midi/4/o/4ocu400dbzrww6uwx37hgf3cfyy2tsx.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/override-midi/4/o/4ocu400dbzrww6uwx37hgf3cfyy2tsx.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Melodic and harmonic intervals</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theory</a>, an <b>interval</b> is a difference in <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a> between two sounds.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> An interval may be described as <b>horizontal</b>, <b>linear</b>, or <b>melodic</b> if it refers to successively sounding tones, such as two adjacent pitches in a melody, and <b>vertical</b> or <b>harmonic</b> if it pertains to simultaneously sounding tones, such as in a <a href="/wiki/Chord_(music)" title="Chord (music)">chord</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LindleyMarkCampbell_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LindleyMarkCampbell-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western</a> music, intervals are most commonly differences between <a href="/wiki/Musical_note" title="Musical note">notes</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a>. Intervals between successive notes of a scale are also known as scale steps. The smallest of these intervals is a <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a>. Intervals smaller than a semitone are called <a href="/wiki/Microtone" class="mw-redirect" title="Microtone">microtones</a>. They can be formed using the notes of various kinds of non-diatonic scales. Some of the very smallest ones are called <a href="/wiki/Comma_(music)" title="Comma (music)">commas</a>, and describe small discrepancies, observed in some <a href="/wiki/Tuning_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuning system">tuning systems</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Enharmonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enharmonic">enharmonically equivalent</a> notes such as C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> and D<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>. Intervals can be arbitrarily small, and even imperceptible to the human ear.
</p><p>In physical terms, an interval is the <a href="/wiki/Interval_ratio" title="Interval ratio">ratio</a> between two sonic frequencies. For example, any two notes an octave apart have a frequency ratio of 2:1. This means that successive increments of pitch by the same interval result in an exponential increase of frequency, even though the human ear perceives this as a linear increase in pitch. For this reason, intervals are often measured in <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">cents</a>, a unit derived from the <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithm</a> of the frequency ratio.
</p><p>In Western music theory, the most common naming scheme for intervals describes two properties of the interval: the <a href="#Quality">quality</a> (perfect, major, minor, augmented, diminished) and <a href="#Number">number</a> (unison, second, third, etc.). Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor third</a> or <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">perfect fifth</a>. These names identify not only the difference in semitones between the upper and lower notes but also how the interval is <a href="/wiki/Enharmonic_spelling" class="mw-redirect" title="Enharmonic spelling">spelled</a>. The importance of spelling stems from the historical practice of differentiating the frequency ratios of enharmonic intervals such as G–G<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> and G–A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Size"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:300px;">
<div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/1/w/1w6120nuipl4edg89h5cgr96aepnkdk/1w6120nu.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/1/w/1w6120nuipl4edg89h5cgr96aepnkdk/1w6120nu.png" width="187" height="52" alt="&#10;\relative c&#39;&#39;{&#10;\hide Staff.TimeSignature&#10;&lt;c c,&gt;1 | c,4 c&#39; c,2&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/1/w/1w6120nuipl4edg89h5cgr96aepnkdk/1w6120nu.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/1/w/1w6120nuipl4edg89h5cgr96aepnkdk/1w6120nu.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Example: Perfect octave on C in equal temperament and just intonation: 2/1 = 1200 cents.</div></div></div>
<p>The size of an interval (also known as its width or height) can be represented using two alternative and equivalently valid methods, each appropriate to a different context: frequency ratios or cents.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequency_ratios">Frequency ratios</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Frequency ratios"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interval_ratio" title="Interval ratio">Interval ratio</a></div>
<p>The size of an interval between two notes may be measured by the <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequencies</a>. When a <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">musical instrument</a> is tuned using a <a href="/wiki/Just_intonation" title="Just intonation">just intonation</a> tuning system, the size of the main intervals can be expressed by small-<a href="/wiki/Integer_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Integer number">integer</a> ratios, such as 1:1 (<a href="/wiki/Unison" title="Unison">unison</a>), 2:1 (<a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a>), 5:3 (<a href="/wiki/Major_sixth" title="Major sixth">major sixth</a>), 3:2 (<a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">perfect fifth</a>), 4:3 (<a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">perfect fourth</a>), 5:4 (<a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major third</a>), 6:5 (<a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor third</a>). Intervals with small-integer ratios are often called <i>just intervals</i>, or <i>pure intervals</i>.
</p><p>Most commonly, however, musical instruments are nowadays tuned using a different tuning system, called <a href="/wiki/12-tone_equal_temperament" class="mw-redirect" title="12-tone equal temperament">12-tone equal temperament</a>. As a consequence, the size of most equal-tempered intervals cannot be expressed by small-integer ratios, although it is very close to the size of the corresponding just intervals. For instance, an <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal-tempered</a> fifth has a frequency ratio of 2<sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac"><span class="num">7</span>&#8260;<span class="den">12</span></span></sup>:1, approximately equal to 1.498:1, or 2.997:2 (very close to 3:2). For a comparison between the size of intervals in different tuning systems, see <a href="#Size_of_intervals_used_in_different_tuning_systems">§&#160;Size of intervals used in different tuning systems</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cents">Cents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cents"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">Cent (music)</a></div>
<p>The standard system for comparing interval sizes is with <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">cents</a>. The cent is a <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic</a> unit of measurement. If frequency is expressed in a <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic scale</a>, and along that scale the distance between a given frequency and its double (also called <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a>) is divided into 1200 equal parts, each of these parts is one cent. In twelve-tone <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a> (12-TET), a tuning system in which all <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitones</a> have the same size, the size of one semitone is exactly 100 cents. Hence, in 12-TET the cent can be also defined as one hundredth of a <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a>.
</p><p>Mathematically, the size in cents of the interval from frequency <i>f</i><sub>1</sub> to frequency <i>f</i><sub>2</sub> is
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n=1200\cdot \log _{2}\left({\frac {f_{2}}{f_{1}}}\right)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>n</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>1200</mn>
        <mo>&#x22C5;<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>log</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
        <mrow>
          <mo>(</mo>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mfrac>
              <msub>
                <mi>f</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>2</mn>
                </mrow>

</math></div><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ab58a62ad77c3cfe5fa9bc589fb1a56698e0505f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display mw-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:21.299ex; height:6.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n=1200\cdot \log _{2}\left({\frac {f_{2}}{f_{1}}}\right)}"></div>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_intervals">Main intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Main intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The table shows the most widely used conventional names for the intervals between the notes of a <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_scale" title="Chromatic scale">chromatic scale</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Perfect_unison" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfect unison">perfect unison</a> (also known as perfect prime)<sup id="cite_ref-prime_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prime-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> is an interval formed by two identical notes. Its size is zero <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">cents</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a> is any interval between two adjacent notes in a chromatic scale, a <a href="/wiki/Whole_tone" class="mw-redirect" title="Whole tone">whole tone</a> is an interval spanning two semitones (for example, a <a href="/wiki/Major_second" title="Major second">major second</a>), and a <a href="/wiki/Tritone" title="Tritone">tritone</a> is an interval spanning three tones, or six semitones (for example, an augmented fourth).<sup id="cite_ref-TritoneA4_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TritoneA4-6">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> Rarely, the term <a href="/wiki/Ditone" title="Ditone">ditone</a> is also used to indicate an interval spanning two whole tones (for example, a <a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major third</a>), or more strictly as a synonym of major third.
</p><p>Intervals with different names may span the same number of semitones, and may even have the same width. For instance, the interval from D to F<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> is a <a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major third</a>, while that from D to G<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> is a <a href="/wiki/Diminished_fourth" title="Diminished fourth">diminished fourth</a>. However, they both span 4 semitones. If the <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">instrument</a> is tuned so that the 12 notes of the chromatic scale are equally spaced (as in <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a>), these intervals also have the same width. Namely, all semitones have a width of 100 <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">cents</a>, and all intervals spanning 4 semitones are 400 cents wide.
</p><p>The names listed here cannot be determined by counting semitones alone. The rules to determine them are explained below. Other names, determined with different naming conventions, are listed in <a href="#Alternative_interval_naming_conventions">a separate section</a>. Intervals <a href="#Minute_intervals">smaller than one semitone</a> (commas or microtones) and <a href="#Compound_intervals">larger than one octave</a> (compound intervals) are introduced below.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<th>Short</th>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interval_number_and_quality">Interval number and quality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Interval number and quality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_intervals_from_C.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Main_intervals_from_C.png/450px-Main_intervals_from_C.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="80" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Main_intervals_from_C.png/675px-Main_intervals_from_C.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Main_intervals_from_C.png/900px-Main_intervals_from_C.png 2x" data-file-width="2184" data-file-height="388" /></a><figcaption>Main intervals from C<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-mwtitle="Intervals.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Intervals.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/af/Intervals.mid/Intervals.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/af/Intervals.mid/Intervals.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>In Western <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theory</a>, an interval is named according to its <i>number</i> (also called <i>diatonic number</i>) and <i>quality</i>. For instance, <i>major third</i> (or <b>M3</b>) is an interval name, in which the term <i>major</i> (<b>M</b>) describes the quality of the interval, and <i>third</i> (<b>3</b>) indicates its number.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number">Number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Number"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg/350px-Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="82" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg/525px-Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg/700px-Staff_lines_and_spaces_SVG.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="100" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Staff_(music)" title="Staff (music)">Staff</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Staff_position" class="mw-redirect" title="Staff position">staff positions</a> indicated</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fifth_C_to_G.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Fifth_C_to_G.png/220px-Fifth_C_to_G.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="77" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Fifth_C_to_G.png/330px-Fifth_C_to_G.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Fifth_C_to_G.png/440px-Fifth_C_to_G.png 2x" data-file-width="2146" data-file-height="748" /></a><figcaption>Fifth from C to G in the A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major scale</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The number of an interval is the number of letter names or <a href="/wiki/Staff_position" class="mw-redirect" title="Staff position">staff positions</a> (lines and spaces) it encompasses, including the positions of both notes forming the interval. For instance, the interval C–G is a fifth (denoted <b>P5</b>) because the notes from C to the G above it encompass five letter names (C, D, E, F, G) and occupy five consecutive staff positions, including the positions of C and G. The <a href="#Main_intervals">table</a> and the figure above show intervals with numbers ranging from 1 (e.g., <b>P1</b>) to 8 (e.g., <b>P8</b>). Intervals with larger numbers are called <a href="#Compound_intervals">compound intervals</a>.
</p><p>There is a <a href="/wiki/One-to-one_correspondence" class="mw-redirect" title="One-to-one correspondence">one-to-one correspondence</a> between staff positions and diatonic-scale <a href="/wiki/Degree_(music)" title="Degree (music)">degrees</a> (the notes of <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
This means that interval numbers can also be determined by counting diatonic scale degrees, rather than staff positions, provided that the two notes that form the interval are drawn from a diatonic scale. Namely, C–G is a fifth because in any diatonic scale that contains C and G, the sequence from C to G includes five notes. For instance, in the A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>-<a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major</a> diatonic scale, the five notes are C–D<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>–F–G (see figure). This is not true for all kinds of scales. For instance, in a <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_scale" title="Chromatic scale">chromatic scale</a>, the notes from C to G are eight (C–C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>–D–D<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>–E–F–F<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>–G). This is the reason interval numbers are also called <i>diatonic numbers</i>, and this convention is called <i>diatonic numbering</i>.
</p><p>If one adds any <a href="/wiki/Accidental_(music)" title="Accidental (music)">accidentals</a> to the notes that form an interval, by definition the notes do not change their staff positions. As a consequence, any interval has the same interval number as the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Natural_(music)" title="Natural (music)">natural</a> interval, formed by the same notes without accidentals. For instance, the intervals C–G<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> (spanning 8 semitones) and C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>–G (spanning 6 semitones) are fifths, like the corresponding natural interval C–G (7 semitones).
</p><p>Notice that interval numbers represent an inclusive count of encompassed staff positions or note names, not the difference between the endpoints. In other words, one starts counting the lower pitch as one, not zero. For that reason, the interval C–C, a perfect unison, is called a prime (meaning "1"), even though there is no difference between the endpoints. Continuing, the interval C–D is a second, but D is only one staff position, or diatonic-scale degree, above C. Similarly, C–E is a third, but E is only two staff positions above C, and so on. As a consequence, joining two intervals always yields an interval number one less than their sum. For instance, the intervals C–E and E–G are thirds, but joined together they form a fifth (C–G), not a sixth. Similarly, a stack of three thirds, such as C–E, E–G, and G–B, is a seventh (C–B), not a ninth.
</p><p>This scheme applies to intervals up to an octave (12 semitones). For larger intervals, see <a href="#Compound_intervals">§&#160;Compound intervals</a> below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality">Quality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Quality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Frame"><a href="/wiki/File:Diatonic_intervals.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Diatonic_intervals.png" decoding="async" width="449" height="273" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="449" data-file-height="273" /></a><figcaption>Intervals formed by the notes of a C major <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The name of any interval is further qualified using the terms <b>perfect</b> (<b>P</b>), <a href="/wiki/Major_and_minor" title="Major and minor">major</a> (<b>M</b>), <a href="/wiki/Major_and_minor" title="Major and minor">minor</a> (<b>m</b>), <a href="/wiki/Augmentation_(music)" title="Augmentation (music)">augmented</a> (<b>A</b>), and <a href="/wiki/Diminution" title="Diminution">diminished</a> (<b>d</b>). This is called its <i>interval quality</i>. It is possible to have doubly diminished and doubly augmented intervals, but these are quite rare, as they occur only in <a href="/wiki/Chromatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromatic">chromatic</a> contexts. The quality of a <a href="#Compound_intervals">compound interval</a> is the quality of the simple interval on which it is based. Some other qualifiers like <i>neutral</i>, <i>subminor</i>, and <i>supermajor</i> are used for <a href="#Non-diatonic_intervals">non-diatonic intervals</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Perfect">Perfect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Perfect"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

C.mid">ⓘ</a></sup></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>Perfect intervals are so-called because they were traditionally considered perfectly consonant,<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
although in Western classical music the perfect fourth was sometimes regarded as a less than perfect consonance, when its function was <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">contrapuntal</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (February 2013)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Conversely, minor, major, augmented or diminished intervals are typically considered less consonant, and were traditionally classified as mediocre consonances, imperfect consonances, or dissonances.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within a <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a><sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> all unisons (<b>P1</b>) and octaves (<b>P8</b>) are perfect. Most fourths and fifths are also perfect (<b>P4</b> and <b>P5</b>), with five and seven semitones respectively. One occurrence of a fourth is augmented (<b>A4</b>) and one fifth is diminished (<b>d5</b>), both spanning six semitones. For instance, in a C-major scale, the <b>A4</b> is between F and B, and the <b>d5</b> is between B and F (see table).
</p><p>By definition, the <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(music)#Intervals" title="Inversion (music)">inversion</a> of a perfect interval is also perfect. Since the inversion does not change the <a href="/wiki/Pitch_class" title="Pitch class">pitch class</a> of the two notes, it hardly affects their level of consonance (matching of their <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_series_(music)" title="Harmonic series (music)">harmonics</a>). Conversely, other kinds of intervals have the opposite quality with respect to their inversion. The inversion of a major interval is a minor interval, the inversion of an augmented interval is a diminished interval.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_and_minor">Major and minor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Major and minor"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Major_and_minor_intervals_on_C.png" class="mw-file-description"><img class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label">M7</span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:Major_seventh_on_C.mid" title="File:Major seventh on C.mid">ⓘ</a></sup></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>As shown in the table, a <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a><sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> defines seven intervals for each interval number, each starting from a different note (seven unisons, seven seconds, etc.). The intervals formed by the notes of a diatonic scale are called diatonic. Except for unisons and octaves, the diatonic intervals with a given interval number always occur in two sizes, which differ by one semitone. For example, six of the fifths span seven semitones. The other one spans six semitones. Four of the thirds span three semitones, the others four. If one of the two versions is a perfect interval, the other is called either diminished (i.e. narrowed by one semitone) or augmented (i.e. widened by one semitone). Otherwise, the larger version is called major, the smaller one minor. For instance, since a 7-semitone fifth is a perfect interval (<b>P5</b>), the 6-semitone fifth is called "diminished fifth" (<b>d5</b>). Conversely, since neither kind of third is perfect, the larger one is called "major third" (<b>M3</b>), the smaller one "minor third" (<b>m3</b>).
</p><p>Within a diatonic scale,<sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> unisons and octaves are always qualified as perfect, fourths as either perfect or augmented, fifths as perfect or diminished, and all the other intervals (seconds, thirds, sixths, sevenths) as major or minor.
</p>

C.mid">ⓘ</a></sup></span>, <span class="ext-phonos"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-39" class="ext-phonos-PhonosButton noexcerpt oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/d\/d7\/Minor_ninth_on_C.mid\/Minor_ninth_on_C.mid.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;label&quot;:{&quot;html&quot;:&quot;A8&quot;},&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;Minor ninth on C.mid&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;noexcerpt&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label">A8</span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:Minor_ninth_on_C.mid" title="File:Minor ninth on C.mid">ⓘ</a></sup></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>Augmented intervals are wider by one semitone than perfect or major intervals, while having the same interval number (i.e., encompassing the same number of staff positions): they are wider by a <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_semitone" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromatic semitone">chromatic semitone</a>. Diminished intervals, on the other hand, are narrower by one semitone than perfect or minor intervals of the same interval number: they are narrower by a chromatic semitone. For instance, an augmented third such as C–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> spans five semitones, exceeding a major third (C–E) by one semitone, while a diminished third such as C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> spans two semitones, falling short of a minor third (C–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>) by one semitone.
</p><p>The augmented fourth (<b>A4</b>) and the diminished fifth (<b>d5</b>) are the only augmented and diminished intervals that appear in diatonic scales<sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> (see table).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Example">Example</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Example"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neither the number, nor the quality of an interval can be determined by counting <a href="/wiki/Semitones" class="mw-redirect" title="Semitones">semitones</a> alone. As explained above, the number of staff positions must be taken into account as well.
</p><p>For example, as shown in the table below, there are four semitones between A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>, between A and C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>, between A and D<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>, and between A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> and E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Doubleflat.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="double flat"><img alt="double flat" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Doubleflat.svg/8px-Doubleflat.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="12" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Doubleflat.svg/12px-Doubleflat.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Doubleflat.svg/16px-Doubleflat.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="133" data-file-height="200" /></a></span></span>, but
</p>
<ul><li>A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>–B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> is a second, as it encompasses two staff positions (A, B), and it is doubly augmented, as it exceeds a major second (such as A–B) by two semitones.</li>
<li>A–C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> is a third, as it encompasses three staff positions (A, B, C), and it is major, as it spans 4 semitones.</li>
<li>A–D<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> is a fourth, as it encompasses four staff positions (A, B, C, D), and it is diminished, as it falls short of a perfect fourth (such as A–D) by one semitone.</li>
<li>A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>-E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Doubleflat.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="double flat"><img alt="double flat" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Doubleflat.svg/8px-Doubleflat.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="12" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Doubleflat.svg/12px-Doubleflat.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Doubleflat.svg/16px-Doubleflat.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="133" data-file-height="200" /></a></span></span> is a fifth, as it encompasses five staff positions (A, B, C, D, E), and it is triply diminished, as it falls short of a perfect fifth (such as A–E) by three semitones.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shorthand_notation">Shorthand notation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Shorthand notation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Intervals are often abbreviated with a <b>P</b> for perfect, <b>m</b> for <a href="/wiki/Major_and_minor" title="Major and minor">minor</a>, <b>M</b> for <a href="/wiki/Major_and_minor" title="Major and minor">major</a>, <b>d</b> for <a href="/wiki/Diminution" title="Diminution">diminished</a>, <b>A</b> for <a href="/wiki/Augmentation_(music)" title="Augmentation (music)">augmented</a>, followed by the interval number. The indications M and P are often omitted. The <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> is P8, and a <a href="/wiki/Unison" title="Unison">unison</a> is usually referred to simply as "a unison" but can be labeled P1. The <a href="/wiki/Tritone" title="Tritone">tritone</a>, an augmented fourth or diminished fifth is often <b>TT</b>. The interval qualities may be also abbreviated with <b>perf</b>, <b>min</b>, <b>maj</b>, <b>dim</b>, <b>aug</b>. Examples:
</p>
<ul><li>m2 (or min2): minor second,</li>
<li>M3 (or maj3): major third,</li>
<li>A4 (or aug4): augmented fourth,</li>
<li>d5 (or dim5): diminished fifth,</li>
<li>P5 (or perf5): perfect fifth.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inversion">Inversion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inversion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(music)#Intervals" title="Inversion (music)">Inversion (music) §&#160;Intervals</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Compound_interval_inversion.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Compound_interval_inversion.png/350px-Compound_interval_inversion.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Compound_interval_inversion.png/525px-Compound_interval_inversion.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Compound_interval_inversion.png/700px-Compound_interval_inversion.png 2x" data-file-width="1064" data-file-height="457" /></a><figcaption>Major 13th (compound major 6th) inverts to a minor 3rd by moving the bottom note up two octaves, the top note down two octaves, or both notes one octave.</figcaption></figure>
<p>A simple interval (i.e., an interval smaller than or equal to an octave) may be <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(music)" title="Inversion (music)">inverted</a> by raising the lower pitch an <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> or lowering the upper pitch an octave. For example, the fourth from a lower C to a higher F may be inverted to make a fifth, from a lower F to a higher C.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/2/t/2tx52nb6dg51i88fqp21wg9jls46581/2tx52nb6.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/2/t/2tx52nb6dg51i88fqp21wg9jls46581/2tx52nb6.png" width="385" height="83" alt="&#10;{&#10;\override Score.TimeSignature&#10;#&#39;stencil = ##f&#10;\override Score.SpacingSpanner.strict-note-spacing = ##t&#10;\set Score.proportionalNotationDuration = #(ly:make-moment 1/4)&#10;\new Staff &lt;&lt;&#10;   \clef treble \time 4/4&#10;   \new Voice \relative c&#39; {&#10;      \stemUp c2 c&#39; c, c&#39; c, c&#39; c, c&#39;&#10;      } &#10;   \new Voice \relative c&#39; {&#10;      \stemDown c2 c d d e e f f&#10;      }&#10;   \addlyrics { &quot;P1&quot; -- &quot;P8&quot; &quot;M2&quot; -- &quot;m7&quot; &quot;M3&quot; -- &quot;m6&quot; &quot;P4&quot; -- &quot;P5&quot; }&#10;&gt;&gt;&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/2/t/2tx52nb6dg51i88fqp21wg9jls46581/2tx52nb6.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/2/t/2tx52nb6dg51i88fqp21wg9jls46581/2tx52nb6.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<p>There are two rules to determine the number and quality of the inversion of any simple interval:<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The interval number and the number of its inversion always add up to nine (4 + 5 = 9, in the example just given).</li>
<li>The inversion of a major interval is a minor interval, and vice versa; the inversion of a perfect interval is also perfect; the inversion of an augmented interval is a diminished interval, and vice versa; the inversion of a doubly augmented interval is a doubly diminished interval, and vice versa.</li></ol>
<p>For example, the interval from C to the E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> above it is a minor third. By the two rules just given, the interval from E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> to the C above it must be a major sixth.
</p><p>Since compound intervals are larger than an octave, "the inversion of any compound interval is always the same as the inversion of the simple interval from which it is compounded".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For intervals identified by their ratio, the inversion is determined by reversing the ratio and multiplying the ratio by 2 until it is greater than 1. For example, the inversion of a 5:4 ratio is an 8:5 ratio.
</p><p>For intervals identified by an integer number of semitones, the inversion is obtained by subtracting that number from 12.
</p><p>Since an <a href="/wiki/Interval_class" title="Interval class">interval class</a> is the lower number selected among the interval integer and its inversion, interval classes cannot be inverted.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Intervals can be described, classified, or compared with each other according to various criteria.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:-moz-fit-content; width:fit-content;"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:auto;">
<div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Melodic_and_harmonic_intervals.mid"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/k/3/k3juf9xtecizrmy0vex01b7193ug9m8/k3juf9xt.png" width="246" height="136" alt="&#10;\layout {&#10;line-width = 60\mm&#10;indent = 0\mm&#10;}&#10;\relative c&#39;&#39;{&#10;\clef treble \time 3/1 \hide Staff.TimeSignature&#10;d,1 g f \bar &quot;||&quot; \break&#10;\time 1/1 &lt;d f&gt; \bar &quot;||&quot; &lt;d g&gt; \bar &quot;||&quot; &lt;f g&gt; \bar &quot;||&quot;&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/override-midi/4/o/4ocu400dbzrww6uwx37hgf3cfyy2tsx.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/override-midi/4/o/4ocu400dbzrww6uwx37hgf3cfyy2tsx.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Melodic and harmonic intervals</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Melodic_and_harmonic">Melodic and harmonic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Melodic and harmonic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Harmony" title="Harmony">Harmony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">Melody</a></div>
<p>An interval can be described as
</p>
<ul><li>Vertical or <a href="/wiki/Harmony" title="Harmony">harmonic</a> if the two notes sound simultaneously</li>
<li>Horizontal, linear, or <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melodic</a> if they sound successively.<sup id="cite_ref-LindleyMarkCampbell_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LindleyMarkCampbell-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Melodic intervals can be <i>ascending</i> (lower pitch precedes higher pitch) or <i>descending</i>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diatonic_and_chromatic">Diatonic and chromatic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Diatonic and chromatic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_and_chromatic" title="Diatonic and chromatic">Diatonic and chromatic</a></div>
<p>In general,
</p>
<ul><li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Diatonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Diatonic">diatonic</a> interval</i> is an interval formed by two notes of a <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a>.</li>
<li>A <i>chromatic interval</i> is a non-diatonic interval formed by two notes of a <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_scale" title="Chromatic scale">chromatic scale</a>.</li></ul>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chromatic_scale_full_octave_ascending_and_descending_on_C.PNG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Chromatic_scale_full_octave_ascending_and_descending_on_C.PNG/500px-Chromatic_scale_full_octave_ascending_and_descending_on_C.PNG" decoding="async" width="500" height="50" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Chromatic_scale_full_octave_ascending_and_descending_on_C.PNG/750px-Chromatic_scale_full_octave_ascending_and_descending_on_C.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Chromatic_scale_full_octave_ascending_and_descending_on_C.PNG 2x" data-file-width="945" data-file-height="95" /></a><figcaption>Ascending and descending chromatic scale on C<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-durationhint="17" data-mwtitle="ChromaticScaleUpDown.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/ChromaticScaleUpDown.ogg/ChromaticScaleUpDown.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/ChromaticScaleUpDown.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>The table <a href="#Quality">above</a> depicts the 56 diatonic intervals formed by the notes of the C major scale (a diatonic scale). Notice that these intervals, as well as any other diatonic interval, can be also formed by the notes of a chromatic scale.
</p><p>The distinction between diatonic and chromatic intervals is controversial, as it is based on the definition of diatonic scale, which is variable in the literature. For example, the interval B–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> (a <a href="/wiki/Diminished_fourth" title="Diminished fourth">diminished fourth</a>, occurring in the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_minor_scale" title="Harmonic minor scale">harmonic C-minor scale</a>) is considered diatonic if the harmonic minor scales are considered diatonic as well.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, it is considered chromatic. For further details, see the <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_and_chromatic" title="Diatonic and chromatic">main article</a>.
</p><p>By a commonly used definition of diatonic scale<sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> (which excludes the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmonic minor">harmonic minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melodic_minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Melodic minor">melodic minor</a> scales), all perfect, major and minor intervals are diatonic. Conversely, no augmented or diminished interval is diatonic, except for the augmented fourth and diminished fifth.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ab_major_scale.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ab_major_scale.png/220px-Ab_major_scale.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="45" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ab_major_scale.png/330px-Ab_major_scale.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ab_major_scale.png/440px-Ab_major_scale.png 2x" data-file-width="1065" data-file-height="220" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/A-flat_major" title="A-flat major">A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>-major</a> scale<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-durationhint="9" data-mwtitle="Ab_major_scale.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/27/Ab_major_scale.mid/Ab_major_scale.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/27/Ab_major_scale.mid/Ab_major_scale.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Ab_major_scale.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>The distinction between diatonic and chromatic intervals may be also sensitive to context. The above-mentioned 56 intervals formed by the C-major scale are sometimes called <i>diatonic to C major</i>. All other intervals are called <i>chromatic to C major</i>. For instance, the perfect fifth A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> is chromatic to C major, because A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> and E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> are not contained in the C major scale. However, it is diatonic to others, such as the A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> major scale.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_and_dissonant">Consonant and dissonant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Consonant and dissonant"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Consonance_and_dissonance" title="Consonance and dissonance">Consonance and dissonance</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Consonance_and_dissonance" title="Consonance and dissonance">Consonance and dissonance</a> are relative terms that refer to the stability, or state of repose, of particular musical effects. Dissonant intervals are those that cause tension and desire to be <i>resolved</i> to consonant intervals.
</p><p>These terms are relative to the usage of different compositional styles.
</p>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_music" title="Renaissance music">15th- and 16th-century</a> usage, perfect fifths and octaves, and major and minor thirds and sixths were considered harmonically consonant, and all other intervals dissonant, including the perfect fourth, which by 1473 was described (by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Tinctoris" title="Johannes Tinctoris">Johannes Tinctoris</a>) as dissonant, except between the upper parts of a vertical sonority—for example, with a supporting third below ("6-3 chords").<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Common_practice_period" title="Common practice period">common practice period</a>, it makes more sense to speak of consonant and dissonant chords, and certain intervals previously considered dissonant (such as minor sevenths) became acceptable in certain contexts. However, 16th-century practice was still taught to beginning musicians throughout this period.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a> (1821–1894) theorised that dissonance was caused by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Beat_(acoustics)" title="Beat (acoustics)">beats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._172:_&quot;The_roughness_from_sounding_two_tones_together_depends..._the_number_of_beats_produced_in_a_second.&quot;_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._172:_&quot;The_roughness_from_sounding_two_tones_together_depends..._the_number_of_beats_produced_in_a_second.&quot;-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Helmholtz further believed that the beating produced by the upper partials of harmonic sounds was the cause of dissonance for intervals too far apart to produce beating between the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_frequency" title="Fundamental frequency">fundamentals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._178:_&quot;The_cause_of_this_phenomenon_must_be_looked_for_in_the_beats_produced_by_the_high_upper_partials_of_such_compound_tones&quot;_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._178:_&quot;The_cause_of_this_phenomenon_must_be_looked_for_in_the_beats_produced_by_the_high_upper_partials_of_such_compound_tones&quot;-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Helmholtz then designated that two harmonic tones that shared common low partials would be more consonant, as they produced less beats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895182_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895182-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Helmholtz disregarded partials above the seventh, as he believed that they were not audible enough to have significant effect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._183:_&quot;Here_I_have_stopped,_because_the_7th_partial_tone_is_entirely_eliminated,_or_at_least_much_weakened.&quot;_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._183:_&quot;Here_I_have_stopped,_because_the_7th_partial_tone_is_entirely_eliminated,_or_at_least_much_weakened.&quot;-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> From this Helmholtz categorises the octave, perfect fifth, perfect fourth, major sixth, major third, and minor third as consonant, in decreasing value, and other intervals as dissonant.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Cope" title="David Cope">David Cope</a> (1997) suggests the concept of <b>interval strength</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cope_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cope-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> in which an interval's strength, consonance, or stability is determined by its approximation to a lower and stronger, or higher and weaker, position in the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_series_(music)" title="Harmonic series (music)">harmonic series</a>. See also: <a href="/wiki/Lipps%E2%80%93Meyer_law" title="Lipps–Meyer law">Lipps–Meyer law</a> and <a href="#Interval_root">#Interval root</a></li></ul>
<p>All of the above analyses refer to vertical (simultaneous) intervals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_and_compound">Simple and compound</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Simple and compound"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_and_compound_major_third.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Simple_and_compound_major_third.png/220px-Simple_and_compound_major_third.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="74" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Simple_and_compound_major_third.png/330px-Simple_and_compound_major_third.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Simple_and_compound_major_third.png/440px-Simple_and_compound_major_third.png 2x" data-file-width="909" data-file-height="306" /></a><figcaption>Simple and compound major third<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_3" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-mwtitle="Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>A simple interval is an interval spanning at most one octave (see <a href="#Main_intervals">Main intervals</a> above). Intervals spanning more than one octave are called compound intervals, as they can be obtained by adding one or more octaves to a simple interval (see <a href="#Compound_intervals">below</a> for details).<sup id="cite_ref-compound_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-compound-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steps_and_skips">Steps and skips</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Steps and skips"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steps_and_skips" title="Steps and skips">Steps and skips</a></div>
<p>Linear (melodic) intervals may be described as <i>steps</i> or <i>skips</i>. A <i>step</i>, or <i>conjunct motion</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bonds_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonds-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
is a linear interval between two consecutive notes of a scale. Any larger interval is called a <i>skip</i> (also called a <i>leap</i>), or <i>disjunct motion</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bonds_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonds-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-diatonic_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diatonic-7">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> a step is either a <a href="/wiki/Minor_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor second">minor second</a> (sometimes also called <i>half step</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Major_second" title="Major second">major second</a> (sometimes also called <i>whole step</i>), with all intervals of a <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor third</a> or larger being skips.
</p><p>For example, C to D (major second) is a step, whereas C to E (<a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major third</a>) is a skip.
</p><p>More generally, a step is a smaller or narrower interval in a musical line, and a skip is a wider or larger interval, where the categorization of intervals into steps and skips is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Musical_tuning" title="Musical tuning">tuning system</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pitch_space" title="Pitch space">pitch space</a> used.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Melodic_motion" title="Melodic motion">Melodic motion</a> in which the interval between any two consecutive pitches is no more than a step, or, less strictly, where skips are rare, is called <i>stepwise</i> or <i>conjunct</i> melodic motion, as opposed to <i>skipwise</i> or <i>disjunct</i> melodic motions, characterized by frequent skips.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enharmonic_intervals">Enharmonic intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Enharmonic intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enharmonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enharmonic">Enharmonic</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Enharmonic_tritone.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Enharmonic_tritone.png/220px-Enharmonic_tritone.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="76" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Enharmonic_tritone.png/330px-Enharmonic_tritone.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Enharmonic_tritone.png/440px-Enharmonic_tritone.png 2x" data-file-width="1411" data-file-height="486" /></a><figcaption>Enharmonic tritones: A4 = d5 on C<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_4" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-durationhint="7" data-mwtitle="Tritone_on_C.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/58/Tritone_on_C.mid/Tritone_on_C.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/58/Tritone_on_C.mid/Tritone_on_C.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Tritone_on_C.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>Two intervals are considered <i><a href="/wiki/Enharmonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enharmonic">enharmonic</a></i>, or <i>enharmonically equivalent</i>, if they both contain the same <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitches</a> spelled in different ways; that is, if the notes in the two intervals are themselves enharmonically equivalent. Enharmonic intervals span the same number of <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitones</a>.
</p><p>For example, the four intervals listed in the table below are all enharmonically equivalent, because the notes F<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> and G<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> indicate the same pitch, and the same is true for A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>. All these intervals span four semitones.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
