<td>&#160;
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>When played as isolated chords on a <a href="/wiki/Piano_keyboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Piano keyboard">piano keyboard</a>, these intervals are indistinguishable to the ear, because they are all played with the same two keys. However, in a musical context, the <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Diatonic function">diatonic function</a> of the notes these intervals incorporate is very different.
</p><p>The discussion above assumes the use of the prevalent tuning system, <a href="/wiki/12-tone_equal_temperament" class="mw-redirect" title="12-tone equal temperament">12-tone equal temperament</a> ("12-TET"). But in other historic <a href="/wiki/Meantone_temperament" title="Meantone temperament">meantone temperaments</a>, the pitches of pairs of notes such as F<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span> and G<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> may not necessarily coincide. These two notes are enharmonic in 12-TET, but may not be so in another tuning system. In such cases, the intervals they form would also not be enharmonic. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Quarter-comma_meantone" title="Quarter-comma meantone">quarter-comma meantone</a>, all four intervals shown in the example above would be different.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Minute_intervals">Minute intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Minute intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png/220px-Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png/330px-Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png/440px-Pythagorean_comma_on_C.png 2x" data-file-width="539" data-file-height="287" /></a><figcaption>Pythagorean comma on C; the note depicted as lower on the staff (B<a href="/wiki/Semitone#Just_intonation" title="Semitone"><span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span></a><a href="/wiki/Syntonic_comma" title="Syntonic comma">+++</a>) is slightly higher in pitch (than C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-natural">&#x266e;</span></span>).<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_5" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-mwtitle="Pythagorean_comma_on_C.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a8/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.mid/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a8/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.mid/Pythagorean_comma_on_C.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Comma_(music)" title="Comma (music)">Comma (music)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microtone" class="mw-redirect" title="Microtone">Microtone</a></div>
<p>There are also a number of minute intervals not found in the chromatic scale or labeled with a diatonic function, which have names of their own. They may be described as <a href="/wiki/Microtone" class="mw-redirect" title="Microtone">microtones</a>, and some of them can be also classified as <a href="/wiki/Comma_(music)" title="Comma (music)">commas</a>, as they describe small discrepancies, observed in some tuning systems, between <a href="/wiki/Enharmonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enharmonic">enharmonically equivalent</a> notes. In the following list, the interval sizes in <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">cents</a> are approximate.
</p>
<ul><li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_comma" title="Pythagorean comma">Pythagorean comma</a></i> is the difference between twelve justly tuned perfect fifths and seven octaves. It is expressed by the <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> ratio 531441:524288 (23.5 cents).</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Syntonic_comma" title="Syntonic comma">syntonic comma</a></i> is the difference between four justly tuned perfect fifths and two octaves plus a major third. It is expressed by the ratio 81:80 (21.5 cents).</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Septimal_comma" title="Septimal comma">septimal comma</a></i> is 64:63 (27.3 cents), and is the difference between the Pythagorean or 3-limit "7th" and the "harmonic 7th".</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Diesis" title="Diesis">diesis</a></i> is generally used to mean the difference between three justly tuned major thirds and one octave. It is expressed by the ratio 128:125 (41.1 cents). However, it has been used to mean other small intervals: see <a href="/wiki/Diesis" title="Diesis">diesis</a> for details.</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Diaschisma" title="Diaschisma">diaschisma</a></i> is the difference between three octaves and four justly tuned perfect fifths plus two justly tuned major thirds. It is expressed by the ratio 2048:2025 (19.6 cents).</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Schisma" title="Schisma">schisma</a></i> (also skhisma) is the difference between five octaves and eight justly tuned fifths plus one justly tuned major third. It is expressed by the ratio 32805:32768 (2.0 cents). It is also the difference between the Pythagorean and syntonic commas. (A schismic major third is a schisma different from a just major third, eight fifths down and five octaves up, F<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> in C.)</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Kleisma" title="Kleisma">kleisma</a></i> is the difference between six <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor thirds</a> and one <i>tritave</i> or <i>perfect twelfth</i> (an <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> plus a <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">perfect fifth</a>), with a frequency ratio of 15625:15552 (8.1 cents) (<span class="ext-phonos"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-40" class="ext-phonos-PhonosButton noexcerpt oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/3\/31\/Kleisma_on_C.mid\/Kleisma_on_C.mid.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;label&quot;:{&quot;html&quot;:&quot;Play&quot;},&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;Kleisma on C.mid&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;noexcerpt&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/3/31/Kleisma_on_C.mid/Kleisma_on_C.mid.mp3" rel="nofollow" aria-label="Play audio" title="Play audio" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label">Play</span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:Kleisma_on_C.mid" title="File:Kleisma on C.mid">ⓘ</a></sup></span>).</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Septimal_kleisma" title="Septimal kleisma">septimal kleisma</a></i> is the amount that two major thirds of 5:4 and a septimal major third, or supermajor third, of 9:7 exceeds the octave. Ratio 225:224 (7.7 cents).</li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Quarter_tone" title="Quarter tone">quarter tone</a></i> is half the width of a <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a>, which is half the width of a <a href="/wiki/Major_second" title="Major second">whole tone</a>. It is equal to exactly 50 cents.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compound_intervals">Compound intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Compound intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_and_compound_major_third.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Simple_and_compound_major_third.png/220px-Simple_and_compound_major_third.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="74" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Simple_and_compound_major_third.png/330px-Simple_and_compound_major_third.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Simple_and_compound_major_third.png/440px-Simple_and_compound_major_third.png 2x" data-file-width="909" data-file-height="306" /></a><figcaption>Simple and compound major third<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_6" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-mwtitle="Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid/Simple_and_compound_major_third.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>A compound interval is an interval spanning more than one octave.<sup id="cite_ref-compound_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-compound-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, intervals spanning at most one octave are called simple intervals (see <a href="#Main_intervals">Main intervals</a> below).
</p><p>In general, a compound interval may be defined by a sequence or "stack" of two or more simple intervals of any kind. For instance, a major tenth (two staff positions above one octave), also called <i>compound major third</i>, spans one octave plus one major third.
</p><p>Any compound interval can be always decomposed into one or more octaves plus one simple interval. For instance, a major seventeenth can be decomposed into two octaves and one major third, and this is the reason why it is called a compound major third, even when it is built by adding up four fifths.
</p><p>The diatonic number <i>DN</i><sub>c</sub> of a compound interval formed from <i>n</i> simple intervals with diatonic numbers <i>DN</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>DN</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>DN</i><sub>n</sub>, is determined by:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle DN_{c}=1+(DN_{1}-1)+(DN_{2}-1)+...+(DN_{n}-1),\ }">

<p>which can also be written as:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle DN_{c}=DN_{1}+DN_{2}+...+DN_{n}-(n-1),\ }">

    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle DN_{c}=DN_{1}+DN_{2}+...+DN_{n}-(n-1),\ }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3ff4dd4717eab97354979da4eb4e2ac92ddbff8d" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:43.752ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle DN_{c}=DN_{1}+DN_{2}+...+DN_{n}-(n-1),\ }"></span></dd></dl>
<p>The quality of a compound interval is determined by the quality of the simple interval on which it is based. For instance, a compound major third is a major tenth (1+(8−1)+(3−1) = 10), or a major seventeenth (1+(8−1)+(8−1)+(3−1) = 17), and a compound perfect fifth is a perfect twelfth (1+(8−1)+(5−1) = 12) or a perfect nineteenth (1+(8−1)+(8−1)+(5−1) = 19). Notice that two octaves are a fifteenth, not a sixteenth (1+(8−1)+(8−1) = 15). Similarly, three octaves are a twenty-second (1+3×(8−1) = 22), and so on.
</p>

<tr>
<td>13</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Minor_ninth" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor ninth">Minor ninth</a></td>

<p>It is also worth mentioning here the major seventeenth (28 semitones)—an interval larger than two octaves that can be considered a multiple of a perfect fifth (7 semitones) as it can be decomposed into four perfect fifths (7 × 4 = 28 semitones), or two octaves plus a major third (12 + 12 + 4 = 28 semitones). Intervals larger than a major seventeenth seldom come up, most often being referred to by their compound names, for example "two octaves plus a fifth"<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> rather than "a 19th".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Intervals_in_chords">Intervals in chords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Intervals in chords"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chord_(music)" title="Chord (music)">Chord (music)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chord_names_and_symbols_(jazz_and_pop_music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)">Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)</a></div>
<p>Chords are sets of three or more notes. They are typically defined as the combination of intervals starting from a common note called the <a href="/wiki/Root_(chord)" title="Root (chord)">root</a> of the chord. For instance a <a href="/wiki/Major_triad" class="mw-redirect" title="Major triad">major triad</a> is a chord containing three notes defined by the root and two intervals (major third and perfect fifth). Sometimes even a single interval (<a href="/wiki/Dyad_(music)" title="Dyad (music)">dyad</a>) is considered a chord.<sup id="cite_ref-Otto_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Otto-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Chords are classified based on the quality and number of the intervals that define them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chord_qualities_and_interval_qualities">Chord qualities and interval qualities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Chord qualities and interval qualities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main chord qualities are <a href="/wiki/Major_chord" title="Major chord">major</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minor_chord" title="Minor chord">minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augmented_chord" class="mw-redirect" title="Augmented chord">augmented</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diminished_seventh_chord" title="Diminished seventh chord">diminished</a>, <a href="/wiki/Half-diminished_seventh_chord" title="Half-diminished seventh chord">half-diminished</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dominant_chord" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominant chord">dominant</a>.
The <a href="/wiki/Chord_symbol" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord symbol">symbols</a> used for chord quality are similar to those used for interval quality (see above). In addition, <b>+</b> or <b>aug</b> is used for augmented, <b>°</b> or <b>dim</b> for diminished, <b><span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><sup>&#248;</sup></span></b> for half diminished, and <b>dom</b> for dominant (the symbol <b>−</b> alone is not used for diminished).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deducing_component_intervals_from_chord_names_and_symbols">Deducing component intervals from chord names and symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Deducing component intervals from chord names and symbols"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main rules to decode chord <i>names or symbols</i> are summarized below. Further details are given at <a href="/wiki/Chord_names_and_symbols_(jazz_and_pop_music)#Rules_to_decode_chord_names_and_symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)">Rules to decode chord names and symbols</a>.
</p>
<ol><li>For 3-note chords (<a href="/wiki/Triad_(music)" title="Triad (music)">triads</a>), <a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major</a> or <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor</a> always refer to the interval of the third above the <a href="/wiki/Root_(chord)" title="Root (chord)">root note</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Augmented_fifth" title="Augmented fifth">augmented</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diminished_fifth" class="mw-redirect" title="Diminished fifth">diminished</a> always refer to the interval of the fifth above root. The same is true for the corresponding symbols (e.g., Cm means C<sup>m3</sup>, and C+ means C<sup>+5</sup>). Thus, the terms third and <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">fifth</a> and the corresponding symbols 3 and 5 are typically omitted. This rule can be generalized to all kinds of chords,<sup id="cite_ref-rule1_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rule1-23">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> provided the above-mentioned qualities appear immediately after the root note, or at the beginning of the chord name or symbol. For instance, in the chord symbols Cm and Cm<sup>7</sup>, m refers to the interval m3, and 3 is omitted. When these qualities do not appear immediately after the root note, or at the beginning of the name or symbol, they should be considered <a href="#Quality">interval qualities</a>, rather than chord qualities. For instance, in Cm<sup>M7</sup> (<a href="/wiki/Minor_major_seventh_chord" title="Minor major seventh chord">minor major seventh chord</a>), m is the chord quality and refers to the m3 interval, while M refers to the M7 interval. When the <a href="#Number">number</a> of an extra interval is specified immediately after chord quality, the quality of that interval may coincide with chord quality (e.g., CM<sup>7</sup> = CM<sup>M7</sup>). However, this is not always true (e.g., Cm<sup>6</sup> = Cm<sup>M6</sup>, C+<sup>7</sup> = C+<sup>m7</sup>, CM<sup>11</sup> = CM<sup>P11</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-rule1_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rule1-23">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Chord_names_and_symbols_(jazz_and_pop_music)#Rules_to_decode_chord_names_and_symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)">main article</a> for further details.</li>
<li>Without contrary information, a <a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major third</a> interval and a <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">perfect fifth</a> interval (<a href="/wiki/Major_triad" class="mw-redirect" title="Major triad">major triad</a>) are implied. For instance, a C chord is a C major triad, and the name C minor seventh (Cm<sup>7</sup>) implies a minor 3rd by rule 1, a perfect 5th by this rule, and a <a href="/wiki/Minor_seventh" title="Minor seventh">minor 7th</a> by definition (see below). This rule has one exception (see next rule).</li>
<li>When the fifth interval is <a href="/wiki/Diminished_fifth" class="mw-redirect" title="Diminished fifth">diminished</a>, the third must be minor.<sup id="cite_ref-rule4_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rule4-24">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> This rule overrides rule 2. For instance, Cdim<sup>7</sup> implies a diminished 5th by rule 1, a minor 3rd by this rule, and a diminished 7th by definition (see below).</li>
<li>Names and symbols that contain only a plain <a href="#Number">interval number</a> (e.g., "seventh chord") or the <a href="/wiki/Root_(chord)" title="Root (chord)">chord root</a> and a number (e.g., "C seventh", or C<sup>7</sup>) are interpreted as follows:
<ul><li>If the number is 2, 4, 6, etc., the chord is a major <a href="/wiki/Added_tone_chord" title="Added tone chord">added tone chord</a> (e.g., C<sup>6</sup> = C<sup>M6</sup> = C<sup>add6</sup>) and contains, together with the implied major triad, an extra <a href="/wiki/Major_second" title="Major second">major 2nd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">perfect 4th</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Major_sixth" title="Major sixth">major 6th</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Chord_names_and_symbols_(jazz_and_pop_music)#Added_tone_chords" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)">names and symbols for added tone chords</a>).</li>
<li>If the number is 7, 9, 11, 13, etc., the chord is <a href="/wiki/Dominant_chord" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominant chord">dominant</a> (e.g., C<sup>7</sup> = C<sup>dom7</sup>) and contains, together with the implied major triad, one or more of the following extra intervals: minor 7th, major 9th, perfect 11th, and major 13th (see names and symbols for <a href="/wiki/Chord_names_and_symbols_(jazz_and_pop_music)#Seventh_chords" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)">seventh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chord_names_and_symbols_(jazz_and_pop_music)#Extended_chords" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord names and symbols (jazz and pop music)">extended chords</a>).</li>
<li>If the number is 5, the chord (technically not a chord in the traditional sense, but a <a href="/wiki/Dyad_(music)" title="Dyad (music)">dyad</a>) is a <a href="/wiki/Power_chord" title="Power chord">power chord</a>. Only the root, a perfect fifth and usually an octave are played.</li></ul></li></ol>
<p>The table shows the intervals contained in some of the main chords (<b>component intervals</b>), and some of the symbols used to denote them. The interval qualities or numbers in <b><a href="/wiki/Boldface" class="mw-redirect" title="Boldface">boldface</a></b> font can be deduced from chord name or symbol by applying rule 1. In symbol examples, C is used as chord root.
</p>

<p>In this table, the interval widths used in four different tuning systems are compared. To facilitate comparison, <a href="/wiki/Just_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Just interval">just intervals</a> as provided by 5-limit tuning (see <a href="/wiki/5-limit_tuning#Twelve_tone_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="5-limit tuning">symmetric scale n.1</a>) are shown in <b><a href="/wiki/Boldface" class="mw-redirect" title="Boldface">bold</a></b> font, and the values in cents are <a href="/wiki/Rounding" title="Rounding">rounded</a> to integers. Notice that in each of the <a href="/wiki/Just_intonation" title="Just intonation">non-equal</a> tuning systems, by definition the width of <i>each</i> type of interval (including the semitone) changes depending on the note that starts the interval. This is the art of <a href="/wiki/Just_intonation" title="Just intonation">just intonation</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a>, the intervals are never precisely in tune with each other. This is the price of using equidistant intervals in a 12-tone scale. For simplicity, for some types of interval the table shows only one value (the <a href="/wiki/Mode_(statistics)" title="Mode (statistics)">most often observed</a> one).
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Quarter-comma_meantone" title="Quarter-comma meantone"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027"><span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>-comma meantone</a>, by definition 11 perfect fifths have a size of approximately 697 cents (700&#160;−&#160;<i>ε</i> cents, where <i>ε</i>&#160;≈&#160;3.42 cents); since the average size of the 12 fifths must equal exactly 700 cents (as in equal temperament), the other one must have a size of about 738 cents (700&#160;+&#160;11<i>ε</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Wolf_fifth" class="mw-redirect" title="Wolf fifth">wolf fifth</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diminished_sixth" title="Diminished sixth">diminished sixth</a>); 8 major thirds have size about 386 cents (400&#160;−&#160;4<i>ε</i>), 4 have size about 427 cents (400&#160;+&#160;8<i>ε</i>, actually <a href="/wiki/Diminished_fourth" title="Diminished fourth">diminished fourths</a>), and their average size is 400 cents. In short, similar differences in width are observed for all interval types, except for unisons and octaves, and they are all multiples of ε (the difference between the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027"><span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>-comma meantone fifth and the average fifth). A more detailed analysis is provided at <a href="/wiki/Quarter-comma_meantone#Size_of_intervals" title="Quarter-comma meantone"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027"><span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>-comma meantone Size of intervals</a>. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027"><span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>-comma meantone was designed to produce just major thirds, but only 8 of them are just (5:4, about 386 cents).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_tuning" title="Pythagorean tuning">Pythagorean tuning</a> is characterized by smaller differences because they are multiples of a smaller <i>ε</i> (<i>ε</i>&#160;≈&#160;1.96 cents, the difference between the Pythagorean fifth and the average fifth). Notice that here the fifth is wider than 700 cents, while in most <a href="/wiki/Meantone_temperament" title="Meantone temperament">meantone temperaments</a>, including <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027"><span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>-comma meantone, it is tempered to a size smaller than 700. A more detailed analysis is provided at <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_tuning#Size_of_intervals" title="Pythagorean tuning">Pythagorean tuning §&#160;Size of intervals</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/5-limit_tuning" class="mw-redirect" title="5-limit tuning">5-limit tuning</a> system uses just tones and semitones as building blocks, rather than a stack of perfect fifths, and this leads to even more varied intervals throughout the scale (each kind of interval has three or four different sizes). A more detailed analysis is provided at <a href="/wiki/5-limit_tuning#Size_of_intervals" class="mw-redirect" title="5-limit tuning">5-limit tuning §&#160;Size of intervals</a>. 5-limit tuning was designed to maximize the number of just intervals, but even in this system some intervals are not just (e.g., 3 fifths, 5 major thirds and 6 minor thirds are not just; also, 3 major and 3 minor thirds are <a href="/wiki/Wolf_interval" title="Wolf interval">wolf intervals</a>).
</p><p>The above-mentioned symmetric scale 1, defined in the 5-limit tuning system, is not the only method to obtain <a href="/wiki/Just_intonation" title="Just intonation">just intonation</a>. It is possible to construct juster intervals or just intervals closer to the equal-tempered equivalents, but most of the ones listed above have been used historically in equivalent contexts. In particular, the <a href="/wiki/5-limit_tuning#Twelve_tone_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="5-limit tuning">asymmetric version</a> of the 5-limit tuning scale provides a juster value for the minor seventh (9:5, rather than 16:9). Moreover, the <a href="/wiki/Tritone" title="Tritone">tritone</a> (augmented fourth or diminished fifth), could have other just ratios; for instance, 7:5 (about 583 cents) or 17:12 (about 603 cents) are possible alternatives for the augmented fourth (the latter is fairly common, as it is closer to the equal-tempered value of 600 cents). The 7:4 interval (about 969 cents), also known as the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_seventh" title="Harmonic seventh">harmonic seventh</a>, has been a contentious issue throughout the history of music theory; it is 31 cents flatter than an equal-tempered minor seventh. For further details about reference ratios, see <a href="/wiki/5-limit_tuning#The_justest_ratios" class="mw-redirect" title="5-limit tuning">5-limit tuning §&#160;The justest ratios</a>.
</p><p>In the diatonic system, every interval has one or more <i><a href="/wiki/Enharmonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enharmonic">enharmonic</a> equivalents</i>, such as <a href="/wiki/Augmented_second" title="Augmented second">augmented second</a> for <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor third</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interval_root">Interval root</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Interval root"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Harmonic_series_intervals.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Harmonic_series_intervals.png/400px-Harmonic_series_intervals.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="171" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Harmonic_series_intervals.png/600px-Harmonic_series_intervals.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Harmonic_series_intervals.png/800px-Harmonic_series_intervals.png 2x" data-file-width="2131" data-file-height="910" /></a><figcaption>Intervals in the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_series_(music)" title="Harmonic series (music)">harmonic series</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Although intervals are usually designated in relation to their lower note, <a href="/wiki/David_Cope" title="David Cope">David Cope</a><sup id="cite_ref-Cope_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cope-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hindemith" title="Paul Hindemith">Hindemith</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> both suggest the concept of <b>interval root</b>. To determine an interval's root, one locates its nearest approximation in the harmonic series. The root of a perfect fourth, then, is its <i>top</i> note because it is an octave of the fundamental in the hypothetical harmonic series. The bottom note of every odd diatonically numbered intervals are the roots, as are the tops of all even numbered intervals. The root of a collection of intervals or a chord is thus determined by the interval root of its strongest interval.
</p><p>As to its usefulness, Cope<sup id="cite_ref-Cope_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cope-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> provides the example of the final tonic chord of some popular music being traditionally analyzable as a "submediant six-five chord" (<a href="/wiki/Added_sixth_chord" class="mw-redirect" title="Added sixth chord">added sixth chords</a> by popular terminology), or a <a href="/wiki/First_inversion" title="First inversion">first inversion</a> seventh chord (possibly the dominant of the mediant V/iii). According to the interval root of the strongest interval of the chord (in first inversion, CEGA), the perfect fifth (C–G), is the bottom C, the tonic.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interval_cycles">Interval cycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Interval cycles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Interval_cycle" title="Interval cycle">Interval cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Identity_(music)" title="Identity (music)">Identity (music)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Interval_cycle" title="Interval cycle">Interval cycles</a>, "unfold [i.e., repeat] a single recurrent interval in a series that closes with a return to the initial pitch class", and are notated by <a href="/wiki/George_Perle" title="George Perle">George Perle</a> using the letter "C", for cycle, with an interval-class integer to distinguish the interval. Thus the diminished-seventh chord would be C3 and the augmented triad would be C4. A superscript may be added to distinguish between transpositions, using 0–11 to indicate the lowest pitch class in the cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Perle_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perle-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_interval_naming_conventions">Alternative interval naming conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Alternative interval naming conventions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As shown below, some of the above-mentioned intervals have alternative names, and some of them take a specific alternative name in <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_tuning" title="Pythagorean tuning">Pythagorean tuning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Five-limit_tuning" title="Five-limit tuning">five-limit tuning</a>, or meantone temperament tuning systems such as <a href="/wiki/Quarter-comma_meantone" title="Quarter-comma meantone">quarter-comma meantone</a>. All the intervals with prefix <i>sesqui-</i> are <a href="/wiki/Just_intonation" title="Just intonation">justly</a> tuned, and their <a href="/wiki/Interval_ratio" title="Interval ratio">frequency ratio</a>, shown in the table, is a <a href="/wiki/Superparticular_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Superparticular number">superparticular number</a> (or epimoric ratio). The same is true for the octave.
</p><p>Typically, a <a href="/wiki/Comma_(music)" title="Comma (music)">comma</a> is a diminished second, but this is not always true (for more details, see <a href="/wiki/Comma_(music)#Alternative_definitions" title="Comma (music)">Alternative definitions of comma</a>). For instance, in <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_tuning" title="Pythagorean tuning">Pythagorean tuning</a> the diminished second is a descending interval (524288:531441, or about −23.5 cents), and the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_comma" title="Pythagorean comma">Pythagorean comma</a> is its opposite (531441:524288, or about 23.5 cents). 5-limit tuning defines <a href="/wiki/Five-limit_tuning#Commas" title="Five-limit tuning">four kinds of comma</a>, three of which meet the definition of diminished second, and hence are listed in the table below. The fourth one, called <a href="/wiki/Syntonic_comma" title="Syntonic comma">syntonic comma</a> (81:80) can neither be regarded as a diminished second, nor as its opposite. See <a href="/wiki/Five-limit_tuning#Diminished_seconds" title="Five-limit tuning">Diminished seconds in 5-limit tuning</a> for further details.
</p>

<p>Additionally, some cultures around the world have their own names for intervals found in their music. For instance, 22 kinds of intervals, called <a href="/wiki/Shruti_(music)" title="Shruti (music)">shrutis</a>, are canonically defined in <a href="/wiki/Indian_classical_music" title="Indian classical music">Indian classical music</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_nomenclature">Latin nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Latin nomenclature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Up to the end of the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> was used as an official language throughout Europe for scientific and music textbooks. In music, many English terms are derived from Latin. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a> is from Latin <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">semitonus</i></span></i>.
</p><p>The prefix semi- is typically used herein to mean "shorter", rather than "half".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Namely, a semitonus, semiditonus, semidiatessaron, semidiapente, semihexachordum, semiheptachordum, or semidiapason, is shorter by one semitone than the corresponding whole interval. For instance, a semiditonus (3 semitones, or about 300 cents) is not half of a ditonus (4 semitones, or about 400 cents), but a ditonus shortened by one semitone. Moreover, in <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_tuning" title="Pythagorean tuning">Pythagorean tuning</a> (the most commonly used tuning system up to the 16th century), a semitritonus (d5) is smaller than a tritonus (A4) by one <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_comma" title="Pythagorean comma">Pythagorean comma</a> (about a quarter of a semitone).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-diatonic_intervals">Non-diatonic intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Non-diatonic intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neutral_interval" title="Neutral interval">Neutral interval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subminor_and_supermajor" title="Subminor and supermajor">Subminor and supermajor</a></div>
<p>Intervals in non-diatonic scales can be named using analogs of the diatonic interval names, by using a diatonic interval of similar size and distinguishing it by varying the quality, or by adding other modifiers. For example, the just interval 7/6 may be referred to as a <i>subminor third</i>, since it is ~267 cents wide, which is narrower than a minor third (300 cents in 12-TET, ~316 cents for the just interval 6/5), or as the <i><a href="/wiki/Septimal_minor_third" title="Septimal minor third">septimal minor third</a></i>, since it is a <a href="/wiki/7-limit" class="mw-redirect" title="7-limit">7-limit</a> interval. These names refer just to the individual interval's size, and the interval number need not correspond to the number of scale degrees of a (heptatonic) scale. This naming is particularly common in <a href="/wiki/Just_intonation" title="Just intonation">just intonation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microtonal" class="mw-redirect" title="Microtonal">microtonal</a> scales.<sup id="cite_ref-xen-extended_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xen-extended-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common of these extended qualities are a <a href="/wiki/Neutral_interval" title="Neutral interval"><b>neutral</b> interval</a>, in between a minor and major interval; and <a href="/wiki/Subminor_and_supermajor" title="Subminor and supermajor"><b>subminor</b> and <b>supermajor</b> intervals</a>, respectively narrower than a minor or wider than a major interval. The exact size of such intervals depends on the tuning system, but they often vary from the diatonic interval sizes by about a <a href="/wiki/Quarter_tone" title="Quarter tone">quarter tone</a> (50 cents, half a chromatic step). For example, the <a href="/wiki/Neutral_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutral second">neutral second</a>, the characteristic interval of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_music" title="Arabic music">Arabic music</a>, in 24-TET is 150 cents, exactly halfway between a minor second and major second. Combined, these yield the progression <i>diminished, subminor, minor, neutral, major, supermajor, augmented</i> for seconds, thirds, sixths, and sevenths. This naming convention can be extended to unisons, fourths, fifths, and octaves with <i>sub</i> and <i>super</i>, yielding the progression <i>diminished, sub, perfect, super, augmented</i>. This allows one to name all intervals in 24-TET or 31-TET, the latter of which was used by <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_Fokker" title="Adriaan Fokker">Adriaan Fokker</a>. Various further extensions are used in <a href="/wiki/Xenharmonic_music" title="Xenharmonic music">Xenharmonic music</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-xen-extended_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xen-extended-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pitch-class_intervals">Pitch-class intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Pitch-class intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Interval_class" title="Interval class">Interval class</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ordered_pitch_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordered pitch interval">Ordered pitch interval</a></div>
<p>In post-tonal or <a href="/wiki/Atonality" title="Atonality">atonal</a> theory, originally developed for equal-tempered European classical music written using the <a href="/wiki/Twelve-tone_technique" title="Twelve-tone technique">twelve-tone technique</a> or <a href="/wiki/Serialism" title="Serialism">serialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Integer_notation" class="mw-redirect" title="Integer notation">integer notation</a> is often used, most prominently in <a href="/wiki/Set_theory_(music)" title="Set theory (music)">musical set theory</a>. In this system, intervals are named according to the number of half steps, from 0 to 11, the largest interval class being 6.
</p><p>In atonal or musical set theory, there are numerous types of intervals, the first being the <a href="/wiki/Ordered_pitch_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordered pitch interval">ordered pitch interval</a>, the distance between two pitches upward or downward. For instance, the interval from C upward to G is 7, and the interval from G downward to C is −7. One can also measure the distance between two pitches without taking into account direction with the unordered pitch interval, somewhat similar to the interval of tonal theory.
</p><p>The interval between pitch classes may be measured with ordered and unordered pitch-class intervals. The ordered one, also called directed interval, may be considered the measure upwards, which, since we are dealing with pitch classes, depends on whichever pitch is chosen as 0. For unordered pitch-class intervals, see <a href="/wiki/Interval_class" title="Interval class">interval class</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generic_and_specific_intervals">Generic and specific intervals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Generic and specific intervals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Specific_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific interval">Specific interval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generic_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Generic interval">Generic interval</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_set_theory" title="Diatonic set theory">diatonic set theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Specific_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific interval">specific</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generic_interval" class="mw-redirect" title="Generic interval">generic intervals</a> are distinguished. Specific intervals are the interval class or number of semitones between scale steps or collection members, and generic intervals are the number of diatonic scale steps (or staff positions) between notes of a collection or scale.
</p><p>Notice that staff positions, when used to determine the conventional interval number (second, third, fourth, etc.), are counted including the position of the lower note of the interval, while generic interval numbers are counted excluding that position. Thus, generic interval numbers are smaller by 1, with respect to the conventional interval numbers.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalizations_and_non-pitch_uses">Generalizations and non-pitch uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Generalizations and non-pitch uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Time-point_series.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Time-point_series.png/260px-Time-point_series.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="170" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Time-point_series.png/390px-Time-point_series.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Time-point_series.png/520px-Time-point_series.png 2x" data-file-width="1078" data-file-height="703" /></a><figcaption>Division of the measure/chromatic scale, followed by pitch/time-point series<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_7" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-mwtitle="Time-point_series.mid" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/74/Time-point_series.mid" type="audio/midi" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/74/Time-point_series.mid/Time-point_series.mid.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/74/Time-point_series.mid/Time-point_series.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>The term "interval" can also be generalized to other music elements besides pitch. <a href="/wiki/David_Lewin" title="David Lewin">David Lewin</a>'s <i>Generalized Musical Intervals and Transformations</i> uses interval as a generic measure of distance between <a href="/wiki/Time-point" class="mw-redirect" title="Time-point">time points</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbres</a>, or more abstract musical phenomena.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For example, an interval between two bell-like sounds, which have no pitch salience, is still perceptible. When two tones have similar acoustic spectra (sets of partials), the interval is just the distance of the shift of a tone spectrum along the frequency axis, so linking to pitches as reference points is not necessary. The same principle naturally applies to pitched tones (with similar harmonic spectra), which means that intervals can be perceived "directly" without pitch recognition. This explains in particular the predominance of <a href="/wiki/Interval_recognition" title="Interval recognition">interval hearing</a> over <a href="/wiki/Absolute_pitch" title="Absolute pitch">absolute pitch</a> hearing.<sup id="cite_ref-Tanguiane1993_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tanguiane1993-34">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tangian1994_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tangian1994-35">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1132942124">.mw-parser-output .portalbox{padding:0;margin:0.5em 0;display:table;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:175px;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .portalborder{border:solid #aaa 1px;padding:0.1em;background:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .portalbox-entry{display:table-row;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;height:1.9em;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portalbox-image{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portalbox-link{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .portalleft{clear:left;float:left;margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portalright{clear:right;float:right;margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}}</style><ul role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portalbox portalborder portalright">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha" style="column-width: 45em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-TritoneA4-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-TritoneA4_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-TritoneA4_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The term <a href="/wiki/Tritone" title="Tritone">tritone</a> is sometimes used more strictly as a synonym of augmented fourth (A4).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-diatonic-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diatonic_7-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
The expression <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a> is herein strictly defined as a <a href="/wiki/Heptatonic_scale" title="Heptatonic scale">7-tone scale</a>, which is either a sequence of successive <a href="/wiki/Natural_note" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural note">natural notes</a> (such as the C-<a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major scale</a>, C–D–E–F–G–A–B, or the A-<a href="/wiki/Minor_scale" title="Minor scale">minor scale</a>, A–B–C–D–E–F–G) or any <a href="/wiki/Transposition_(music)" title="Transposition (music)">transposition</a> thereof. In other words, a scale that can be written using seven consecutive notes without <a href="/wiki/Accidental_(music)" title="Accidental (music)">accidentals</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Staff_(music)" title="Staff (music)">staff</a> with a conventional <a href="/wiki/Key_signature" title="Key signature">key signature</a>, or with no signature. This includes, for instance, the <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Natural_minor_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural minor scale">natural minor</a> scales, but does not include some other seven-tone scales, such as the <a href="/wiki/Melodic_minor_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Melodic minor scale">melodic minor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_minor_scale" title="Harmonic minor scale">harmonic minor</a> scales (see also <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_and_chromatic" title="Diatonic and chromatic">Diatonic and chromatic</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-rule1-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-rule1_23-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-rule1_23-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">General rule 1 achieves consistency in the interpretation of symbols such as CM<sup>7</sup>, Cm<sup>6</sup>, and C+<sup>7</sup>. Some musicians legitimately prefer to think that, in CM<sup>7</sup>, M refers to the seventh, rather than to the third. This alternative approach is legitimate, as both the third and seventh are major, yet it is inconsistent, as a similar interpretation is impossible for Cm<sup>6</sup> and C+<sup>7</sup> (in Cm<sup>6</sup>, m cannot possibly refer to the sixth, which is major by definition, and in C+<sup>7</sup>, + cannot refer to the seventh, which is minor). Both approaches reveal only one of the intervals (M3 or M7), and require other rules to complete the task. Whatever is the decoding method, the result is the same (e.g., CM<sup>7</sup> is always conventionally decoded as C–E–G–B, implying M3, P5, M7). The advantage of rule 1 is that it has no exceptions, which makes it the simplest possible approach to decode chord quality. <div class="paragraphbreak" style="margin-top:0.5em"></div>
<p>According to the two approaches, some may format the major seventh chord as CM<sup>7</sup> (general rule 1: M refers to M3), and others as C<sup>M7</sup> (alternative approach: M refers to M7). Fortunately, even C<sup>M7</sup> becomes compatible with rule 1 if it is considered an abbreviation of CM<sup>M7</sup>, in which the first M is omitted. The omitted M is the quality of the third, and is deduced according to rule 2 (see above), consistently with the interpretation of the plain symbol C, which by the same rule stands for CM.
</p>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-rule4-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-rule4_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All triads are <a href="/wiki/Chord_(music)#Secundal,_tertian,_and_quartal_chords" title="Chord (music)">tertian</a> chords (chords defined by sequences of thirds), and a major third would produce in this case a non-tertian chord. Namely, the diminished fifth spans 6 semitones from root, thus it may be decomposed into a sequence of two <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor thirds</a>, each spanning 3 semitones (m3 + m3), compatible with the definition of tertian chord. If a major third were used (4 semitones), this would entail a sequence containing a major second (M3 + M2 = 4 + 2 semitones = 6 semitones), which would not meet the definition of tertian chord.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interval_(music)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: References"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._172:_&quot;The_roughness_from_sounding_two_tones_together_depends..._the_number_of_beats_produced_in_a_second.&quot;-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._172:_&quot;The_roughness_from_sounding_two_tones_together_depends..._the_number_of_beats_produced_in_a_second.&quot;_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHelmholtz1895">Helmholtz 1895</a>, p. 172: "The roughness from sounding two tones together depends... the number of beats produced in a second.".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._178:_&quot;The_cause_of_this_phenomenon_must_be_looked_for_in_the_beats_produced_by_the_high_upper_partials_of_such_compound_tones&quot;-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._178:_&quot;The_cause_of_this_phenomenon_must_be_looked_for_in_the_beats_produced_by_the_high_upper_partials_of_such_compound_tones&quot;_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHelmholtz1895">Helmholtz 1895</a>, p. 178: "The cause of this phenomenon must be looked for in the beats produced by the high upper partials of such compound tones".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895182-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895182_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHelmholtz1895">Helmholtz 1895</a>, p.&#160;182.</span>

<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Helmholtz, Hermann L. F.</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Sensations_of_Tone" title="Sensations of Tone">On the Sensations of Tone as a Theoretical Basis for the Theory of Music</a></i>, 2nd English edition, translated by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_John_Ellis" title="Alexander John Ellis">Ellis, Alexander J.</a> (1885) reprinted by Dover Publications with new introduction (1954) <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-486-60753-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-486-60753-4">0-486-60753-4</a>, p. 182d: "Just as the coincidences of the two first upper partial tones led us to the natural consonances of the Octave and Fifth, the coincidences of higher upper partials would lead us to a further series of natural consonances."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._183:_&quot;Here_I_have_stopped,_because_the_7th_partial_tone_is_entirely_eliminated,_or_at_least_much_weakened.&quot;-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelmholtz1895p._183:_&quot;Here_I_have_stopped,_because_the_7th_partial_tone_is_entirely_eliminated,_or_at_least_much_weakened.&quot;_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHelmholtz1895">Helmholtz 1895</a>, p. 183: "Here I have stopped, because the 7th partial tone is entirely eliminated, or at least much weakened.".</span>

<li id="cite_note-xen-extended-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-xen-extended_30-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-xen-extended_30-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://en.xen.wiki/w/Extended-diatonic_interval_names">"Extended-diatonic interval names"</a>. <i>Xenharmonic wiki</i>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Xenharmonic+wiki&amp;rft.atitle=Extended-diatonic+interval+names&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fen.xen.wiki%2Fw%2FExtended-diatonic_interval_names&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AInterval+%28music%29" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFRoeder2001" class="citation encyclopaedia cs1">Roeder, John (2001). "Interval Class". In <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Sadie" title="Stanley Sadie">Sadie, Stanley</a>; <a href="/wiki/John_Tyrrell_(musicologist)" title="John Tyrrell (musicologist)">Tyrrell, John</a> (eds.). <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Grove_Dictionary_of_Music_and_Musicians" title="The New Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians">The New Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians</a></i> (2nd&#160;ed.). </li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/David_Lewin" title="David Lewin">Lewin, David</a> (1987). <i>Generalized Musical Intervals and Transformations</i>, for example sections 3.3.1 and 5.4.2. New Haven: Yale University Press. Reprinted Oxford University Press, 2007. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-531713-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-531713-8">978-0-19-531713-8</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ockelford, Adam (2005). <i>Repetition in Music: Theoretical and Metatheoretical Perspectives</i>, p.&#160;7. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7546-3573-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-7546-3573-2">0-7546-3573-2</a>. "Lewin posits the notion of musical 'spaces' made up of elements between which we can intuit 'intervals'....Lewin gives a number of examples of musical spaces, including the diatonic gamut of pitches arranged in scalar order; the 12 pitch classes under equal temperament; a succession of time-points pulsing at regular temporal distances one time unit apart; and a family of durations, each measuring a temporal span in time units....transformations of timbre are proposed that derive from changes in the spectrum of partials..."</span>
</li>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-Tangian1994-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Tangian1994_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFTanguiane_(Tangian)1994" class="citation journal cs1"><a href="/wiki/Andranik_Tangian" title="Andranik Tangian">Tanguiane (Tangian), Andranick</a> (1994).
